<meta name="DC.Title" content="Subgenome Dominance in an Interspecific Hybrid, Synthetic Allopolyploid, and a 140-Year-Old Naturally Established Neo-Allopolyploid Monkeyflower" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1105/tpc.17.00010" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2017-09-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Society of Plant Biologists" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="© 2017 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Recent studies have shown that one of the parental subgenomes in ancient polyploids is generally more dominant, having retained more genes and being more highly expressed, a phenomenon termed subgenome dominance. The genomic features that determine how quickly and which subgenome dominates within a newly formed polyploid remain poorly understood. To investigate the rate of emergence of subgenome dominance, we examined gene expression, gene methylation, and transposable element ([TE][1]) methylation in a natural, &lt;140-year-old allopolyploid ( Mimulus peregrinus ), a resynthesized interspecies triploid hybrid ( M. robertsii ), a resynthesized allopolyploid ( M. peregrinus ), and progenitor species ( M. guttatus and M. luteus ). We show that subgenome expression dominance occurs instantly following the hybridization of divergent genomes and significantly increases over generations. Additionally, CHH methylation levels are reduced in regions near genes and within TEs in the first-generation hybrid, intermediate in the resynthesized allopolyploid, and are repatterned differently between the dominant and recessive subgenomes in the natural allopolyploid. Subgenome differences in levels of [TE][1] methylation mirror the increase in expression bias observed over the generations following hybridization. These findings provide important insights into genomic and epigenomic shock that occurs following hybridization and polyploid events and may also contribute to uncovering the mechanistic basis of heterosis and subgenome dominance.

*   ### Glossary
    
    WGD
    :   whole-genome duplication
    TE
    :   transposable element
    LRT
    :   likelihood ratio test
    CDS
    :   coding sequence
    TSD
    :   target-site duplication

 [1]: #def-2" />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Recent studies have shown that one of the parental subgenomes in ancient polyploids is generally more dominant, having retained more genes and being more highly expressed, a phenomenon termed subgenome dominance. The genomic features that determine how quickly and which subgenome dominates within a newly formed polyploid remain poorly understood. To investigate the rate of emergence of subgenome dominance, we examined gene expression, gene methylation, and transposable element (TE) methylation in a natural, &amp;lt;140-year-old allopolyploid (&lt;i&gt;Mimulus peregrinus&lt;/i&gt;), a resynthesized interspecies triploid hybrid (&lt;i&gt;M. robertsii&lt;/i&gt;), a resynthesized allopolyploid (&lt;i&gt;M. peregrinus&lt;/i&gt;), and progenitor species (&lt;i&gt;M. guttatus&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;M. luteus&lt;/i&gt;). We show that subgenome expression dominance occurs instantly following the hybridization of divergent genomes and significantly increases over generations. Additionally, CHH methylation levels are reduced in regions near genes and within TEs in the first-generation hybrid, intermediate in the resynthesized allopolyploid, and are repatterned differently between the dominant and recessive subgenomes in the natural allopolyploid. Subgenome differences in levels of TE methylation mirror the increase in expression bias observed over the generations following hybridization. These findings provide important insights into genomic and epigenomic shock that occurs following hybridization and polyploid events and may also contribute to uncovering the mechanistic basis of heterosis and subgenome dominance.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Subgenome Dominance in an Interspecific Hybrid, Synthetic Allopolyploid, and a 140-Year-Old Naturally Established Neo-Allopolyploid Monkeyflower" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Recent studies have shown that one of the parental subgenomes in ancient polyploids is generally more dominant, having retained more genes and being more highly expressed, a phenomenon termed subgenome dominance. The genomic features that determine how quickly and which subgenome dominates within a newly formed polyploid remain poorly understood. To investigate the rate of emergence of subgenome dominance, we examined gene expression, gene methylation, and transposable element ([TE][1]) methylation in a natural, &lt;140-year-old allopolyploid ( Mimulus peregrinus ), a resynthesized interspecies triploid hybrid ( M. robertsii ), a resynthesized allopolyploid ( M. peregrinus ), and progenitor species ( M. guttatus and M. luteus ). We show that subgenome expression dominance occurs instantly following the hybridization of divergent genomes and significantly increases over generations. Additionally, CHH methylation levels are reduced in regions near genes and within TEs in the first-generation hybrid, intermediate in the resynthesized allopolyploid, and are repatterned differently between the dominant and recessive subgenomes in the natural allopolyploid. Subgenome differences in levels of [TE][1] methylation mirror the increase in expression bias observed over the generations following hybridization. These findings provide important insights into genomic and epigenomic shock that occurs following hybridization and polyploid events and may also contribute to uncovering the mechanistic basis of heterosis and subgenome dominance.

*   ### Glossary
    
    WGD
    :   whole-genome duplication
    TE
    :   transposable element
    LRT
    :   likelihood ratio test
    CDS
    :   coding sequence
    TSD
    :   target-site duplication

 [1]: #def-2" />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Recent studies have shown that one of the parental subgenomes in ancient polyploids is generally more dominant, having retained more genes and being more highly expressed, a phenomenon termed subgenome dominance. The genomic features that determine how quickly and which subgenome dominates within a newly formed polyploid remain poorly understood. To investigate the rate of emergence of subgenome dominance, we examined gene expression, gene methylation, and transposable element ([TE][1]) methylation in a natural, &lt;140-year-old allopolyploid ( Mimulus peregrinus ), a resynthesized interspecies triploid hybrid ( M. robertsii ), a resynthesized allopolyploid ( M. peregrinus ), and progenitor species ( M. guttatus and M. luteus ). We show that subgenome expression dominance occurs instantly following the hybridization of divergent genomes and significantly increases over generations. Additionally, CHH methylation levels are reduced in regions near genes and within TEs in the first-generation hybrid, intermediate in the resynthesized allopolyploid, and are repatterned differently between the dominant and recessive subgenomes in the natural allopolyploid. Subgenome differences in levels of [TE][1] methylation mirror the increase in expression bias observed over the generations following hybridization. These findings provide important insights into genomic and epigenomic shock that occurs following hybridization and polyploid events and may also contribute to uncovering the mechanistic basis of heterosis and subgenome dominance.

    :   whole-genome duplication
    TE
    :   transposable element
    LRT
    :   likelihood ratio test
    CDS

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="68544" id="node68544--2" data-pisa="plantcell;29/9/2150" data-pisa-master="plantcell;tpc.17.00010" data-apath="/plantcell/29/9/2150.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-jcore-article-title-compact clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">
  
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-overline"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-article-type highwire-cite-metadata">Research Article</span><span class="separator-pipe"></span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-art-cat highwire-cite-metadata"><span class="wrapper">Research Article</span></span></div>
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-access"><span class="highwire-citation-access"><i class="highwire-access-icon highwire-access-icon-open-access open-access icon-unlock-alt" title="Open Access" aria-hidden="true"></i><span  class="element-invisible highwire-access-icon highwire-access-icon-open-access" style="">Open Access</span></span></div>
  
      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">Subgenome Dominance in an Interspecific Hybrid, Synthetic Allopolyploid, and a 140-Year-Old Naturally Established Neo-Allopolyploid Monkeyflower</h1>  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-authors"><span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Patrick P. Edger</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Ronald Smith</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="2">Michael R. McKain</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="3">Arielle M. Cooley</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="4">Mario Vallejo-Marin</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="5">Yaowu Yuan</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="6">Adam J. Bewick</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="7">Lexiang Ji</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="8">Adrian E. Platts</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="9">Megan J. Bowman</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="10">Kevin L. Childs</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="11">Jacob D. Washburn</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="12">Robert J. Schmitz</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="13">Gregory D. Smith</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="14">J. Chris Pires</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="15">Joshua R. Puzey</span></span></div>
  
  
  
  
</div>

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">© 2017 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.</li></ul><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-7">Recent studies have shown that one of the parental subgenomes in ancient polyploids is generally more dominant, having retained more genes and being more highly expressed, a phenomenon termed subgenome dominance. The genomic features that determine how quickly and which subgenome dominates within a newly formed polyploid remain poorly understood. To investigate the rate of emergence of subgenome dominance, we examined gene expression, gene methylation, and transposable element (<a id="xref-def-2-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a>) methylation in a natural, &lt;140-year-old allopolyploid (<em>Mimulus peregrinus</em>), a resynthesized interspecies triploid hybrid (<em>M. robertsii</em>), a resynthesized allopolyploid (<em>M. peregrinus</em>), and progenitor species (<em>M. guttatus</em> and <em>M. luteus</em>). We show that subgenome expression dominance occurs instantly following the hybridization of divergent genomes and significantly increases over generations. Additionally, CHH methylation levels are reduced in regions near genes and within TEs in the first-generation hybrid, intermediate in the resynthesized allopolyploid, and are repatterned differently between the dominant and recessive subgenomes in the natural allopolyploid. Subgenome differences in levels of <a id="xref-def-2-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> methylation mirror the increase in expression bias observed over the generations following hybridization. These findings provide important insights into genomic and epigenomic shock that occurs following hybridization and polyploid events and may also contribute to uncovering the mechanistic basis of heterosis and subgenome dominance.</p></div><div class="section introduction" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">INTRODUCTION</h2><p id="p-13">Whole-genome duplications (WGDs) have been an important recurrent process throughout the evolutionary history of eukaryotes (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">McLysaght et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Dehal and Boore, 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Otto, 2007</a>), including having contributed to the origin of novel traits and shifts in net diversification rates (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Levin, 1983</a>; <a id="xref-ref-99-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Wright et al., 1998</a>; <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Crow and Wagner, 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Chao et al., 2013</a>; <a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Edger et al., 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-87-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Tank et al., 2015</a>). WGDs are especially widespread across flowering plants (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">Cui et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-94-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">Vanneste et al., 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">Soltis and Soltis, 2016</a>), with both deep <a id="xref-def-1-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> events (all extant angiosperms share at least two events; <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Jiao et al., 2011</a>) and a plethora of more recent events including those unique to our model system, <em>Mimulus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-93-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">Vallejo-Marín et al., 2015</a>). Polyploids, species that have three or more complete sets of genomes, are grouped into two main categories: autopolyploids (<a id="xref-def-1-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> that occurred within a species) and allopolyploids (<a id="xref-def-1-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> coupled with an interspecific hybridization) (<a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Ramsey and Schemske, 1998</a>). Previous studies indicate that allopolyploids are more likely to persist and become ecologically established, a fact that has partially been attributed to heterosis due to transgressive gene expression and fixed heterozygosity (<a id="xref-ref-55-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">McLysaght et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Crow and Wagner, 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Rapp et al., 2009</a>; Barker et al., 2016). It is worth noting that this finding may be affected by the fact that it is often easier to identify allopolyploids than autopolyploids.</p><p id="p-14">Newly formed allopolyploids face the unique challenge of organizing two genomes (i.e., subgenomes), each contributed by different parental species, that have independently evolved in separate contexts, but which now exist within a single nucleus (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Comai, 2005</a>). Homoploid hybridization and allopolyploidization may disrupt both genetic and epigenomic processes resulting in altered DNA methylation patterns (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">Shaked et al., 2001</a>; <a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Mittelsten Scheid et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Salmon et al., 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Lukens et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Chen, 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">Song and Chen, 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Rigal et al., 2016</a>), changes in gene expression (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Adams et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Adams and Wendel, 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Buggs et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Chelaifa et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Coate et al., 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Renny-Byfield et al., 2015</a>, <a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">2017</a>) and transposable element (<a id="xref-def-2-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a>) reactivation (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Dion-Côté et al., 2014</a>), commonly referred to as genomic shock (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">McClintock, 1984</a>). These genome-wide changes are associated with novel phenotypic variation in newly formed allopolyploids (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Madlung et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Gaeta et al., 2007</a>), which likely contributed to the survival and ultimate success of polyploids (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Kagale et al., 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-94-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">Vanneste et al., 2014</a>).</p><p id="p-15">One observation that may be linked to the long-term success of allopolyploids is that homoeologous genes (orthologous genes encoded on different parental subgenomes) are often expressed at nonequal levels, with genome-wide expression abundance patterns being highly skewed toward one of the subgenomes. Examples of plants with evidence for subgenome-specific expression include maize (<em>Zea mays</em>; <a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">Schnable et al., 2011</a>), <em>Brassica</em> (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">Cheng et al., 2016</a>), cotton (<em>Gossypium</em> spp; <a id="xref-ref-66-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Renny-Byfield et al., 2015</a>), wheat (<em>Triticum aestivum</em>; <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Li et al., 2014</a>), <em>Tragopogon</em> (<a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Buggs et al., 2010</a>), <em>Spartina</em> (<a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Chelaifa et al., 2010</a>), and <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-ref-95-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">Wang et al., 2004</a>). Additionally, it has been shown that the less expressed subgenome tends to be more highly fractionated (i.e., accumulate more deletions), a pattern thought to be due to relaxed selective constraints. Collectively, these phenomena are referred to as “subgenome dominance” (<a id="xref-ref-98-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-98">Woodhouse et al., 2010</a>). For example, in <em>Brassica rapa</em>, a three-way battle ensued following a whole-genome triplication event that occurred over ten million years ago resulting in a single dominant subgenome emerging and two highly fractionated subgenomes (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Tang et al., 2012</a>). The most highly fractionated subgenome lost more than double the total number of genes than the least fractionated, dominant subgenome.</p><p id="p-16">It remains largely unknown how one subgenome becomes more highly expressed, with respect to either whole genome patterns or specific genes. Another unanswered question is, on what time scale (i.e., how quickly) does subgenome dominance become established? Subgenome dominance in newly formed hybrids and allopolyploids could have substantial implications for our understanding of plant hybridization in both ecological and agricultural contexts. In addition, a mechanistic understanding of these phenomena is fundamental to better understanding the long-term evolutionary advantages of WGDs. One hint at a mechanism may be that gene expression can be impacted by the proximity to and methylation status of nearby TEs (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Hollister and Gaut, 2009</a>). Prompted by the finding that the density of methylated TEs is negatively correlated with gene expression magnitude, <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Freeling et al. (2012)</a> hypothesized that the relationship between <a id="xref-def-2-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> repression and the expression of neighboring genes might explain patterns of observed subgenome dominance. The degree of methylation repatterning and reestablishment genome-wide, specifically nearby genes, following hybridization and/or <a id="xref-def-1-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> is largely unknown. Here, we tested this hypothesis by assessing (1) the overall rate that subgenome expression dominance is established following hybridization and <a id="xref-def-1-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a>, (2) genome-wide methylation repatterning following hybridization and <a id="xref-def-1-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a>, and (3) the influence of methylation repatterning on biased expression of parental subgenomes.</p><p id="p-17">Research in most polyploid systems is hindered by at least one of two major difficulties: (1) lack of genomic resources for extant parental progenitors (if parents are known) or (2) the inability to confidently partition the polyploid genome to each of the parental subgenomes. Here, we used the recently and recurrently formed natural allopolyploid, <em>Mimulus peregrinus</em>, to overcome these hurdles. <em>M. peregrinus</em> (6x) is derived from the hybridization of <em>M. luteus</em> (4x) and <em>M. guttatus</em> (2x), which produced a sterile triploid intermediate <em>M. x robertsii</em> (3x) that underwent a subsequent <a id="xref-def-1-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> to regain fertility (<a id="xref-ref-91-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">Vallejo-Marín, 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-93-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">Vallejo-Marín et al., 2015</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1</a>). Importantly, <em>M. luteus</em> (native to Chile) and <em>M. guttatus</em> (native to Western North America) only recently came into contact following a documented introduction into the UK in the early 1800s (<a id="xref-ref-93-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">Vallejo-Marín et al., 2015</a>). Thus, we have a narrow time window for the formation of <em>M. peregrinus</em>. Moreover, the natural allopolyploid (<em>M. peregrinus</em>) still exists with its introduced parents in the UK, which allows us to recreate hybrids and synthetic allopolyploids in the lab. Furthermore, the <em>M. guttatus</em> genome was recently published (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Hellsten et al., 2013</a>), and we complement this with a new genome assembly for <em>M. luteus</em>. <em>M. luteus</em> is an allopolyploid formed from two unknown diploid progenitors, which may be long extinct due to the uncertain age of the polyploid event. These resources and the unique natural history of <em>M. peregrinus</em> have provided an unprecedented opportunity to properly investigate patterns of homoeolog expression bias (measured by the ratio of expression of the homoeologs) across subgenomes in a neo-allopolyploid and its relation to DNA methylation and <a id="xref-def-2-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> density differences.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Whole-Genome Duplications in Mimulus and Related Species. The allotetraploid <em>M. luteus</em> has two distinct subgenomes; <em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and <em>L<sub>b</sub></em> represent the two distinct subgenomes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div class="section results" id="sec-2"><h2 class="">RESULTS</h2><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3><em>M. luteus</em> Genome Assembly</h3><p id="p-22">Here, we present the draft genome of <em>M. luteus</em> with a total genome size estimate of 640 to 680 Mb based on flow cytometry and kmer spectrum analysis (<a id="xref-ref-91-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">Vallejo-Marín, 2012</a>). We sequenced the genome of an inbred line (EY7). The assembly contains 6439 scaffolds spanning 410 Mb with an N50 of 283 kb, representing roughly 60% of the genome, with gene content analyses supporting the recovery of nearly the entire gene space. A total of 46,855 protein-coding genes were annotated in <em>M. luteus</em> genome, which is nearly double the number of protein coding genes (26,718) previously annotated in the <em>M. guttatus</em> genome (430 Mb estimated; 300 Mb assembled) (<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Hellsten et al., 2013</a>). This difference in gene content supports a tetraploid event unique to <em>M. luteus</em>, further supported by a 2:1 syntenic block ratio when compared with the <em>M. guttatus</em> genome, plus base chromosome number differences between these species (<a id="xref-ref-91-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">Vallejo-Marin, 2012</a>). The vast majority of BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) (<a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">Simão et al., 2015</a>) groups, 931 of 956 (97.4%), were identified in the <em>M. luteus</em> genome assembly with 837 duplicates. The high number of identified duplicates supports both the tetraploid event and assembly completeness of the genic regions from both subgenomes. We mapped all mate pair reads back to the <em>M. luteus</em> assembly to screen for possible chimeric contigs or scaffolds between the two subgenomes (<em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and <em>L<sub>b</sub></em>). No instance of chimeric fusions between subgenomes was identified in the assembly (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.17.00010/DC1">Supplemental Figures 1 and 2</a>), which was largely enabled by the overall sequence divergence of the two subgenomes (genome-wide average = 89.33% sequence similarity across genes) and leveraging overlapping paired-end sequences as single long reads. We reannotated the <em>M. guttatus</em> genome with identical methods used for <em>M. luteus</em>, reducing the total number to 25,465 protein coding genes. The reannotation of this genome permits us to make proper genomic and transcriptomic comparisons by removing artifacts that arise due to differences in genome annotation pipelines. A total of 319,944 and 451,448 TEs or <a id="xref-def-2-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> fragments were annotated in the <em>M. guttatus</em> and <em>M. luteus</em> genomes, respectively. To annotate TEs, the <a id="xref-def-2-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> exemplar library (see Methods) was used to identify TEs in the <em>M. guttatus</em> and <em>M. luteus</em> genomes using RepeatMasker. We combined these two genomes to represent <em>M. peregrinus</em>.</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>History of <a id="xref-def-1-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> in <em>Mimulus</em></h3><p id="p-23">A shared ancient whole-genome duplication was detected in both genomes, termed Mimulus-alpha, with a mean Ks of 0.92 and phylogenetically placed at the most recent common ancestor of Mimulus (Phrymaceae) and nearly all other Lamiales families (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.17.00010/DC1">Supplemental Figures 3 to 5</a>). Support was found at the base of the species tree for two possible <a id="xref-def-1-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> events: one prior to the diversification of all sampled Lamiales species and one after the divergence of Oleaceae. The PUG algorithm estimates the timing of <a id="xref-def-1-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> by identifying when coalescence of paralogs occurs in the context of a species tree. It is possible that the putative event placed after the divergence of Oleaceae is the result of artifactual gene tree reconciliation to the species tree. Another possibility is the event after the divergence of Oleaceae is the result of hybridization between the Oleaceae and the rest of Lamiales as suggested by <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Julca et al. (2017)</a>. Follow-up studies are needed to verify these findings with additional species sampling. A recent mean-date estimate for that phylogenetic node is 71 million years before present (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Magallón et al., 2015</a>), while other studies have obtained earlier and later date estimates (<a id="xref-ref-87-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Tank et al., 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-97-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">Wikström et al., 2015</a>). Our taxon sampling did not include the earliest diverging lineage (family Plocospermataceae) in Lamiales (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Refulio-Rodriguez and Olmstead, 2014</a>; <a id="xref-ref-49-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Magallón et al., 2015</a>; <a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">Stull et al., 2015</a>). The Mimulus-alpha event is shared by all other surveyed Lamiales families. A total of 757 unique shared duplications from Mimulus-alpha were identified in all surveyed taxa. Two additional duplication events detected across Lamiales were not shared with Phrymaceae. The first detected event was shared by Orobanche (Orobanchaceae) and Striga (Oleaceae) and was supported by 1738 shared duplicate pairs. The second <a id="xref-def-1-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> event was shared by Olea and Fraxinus and supported by 3312 shared duplicate pairs.</p><p id="p-24"><em>M. luteus</em> experienced an additional whole genome duplication event not shared with <em>M. guttatus</em>. As a result, for every <em>M. guttatus</em> gene, <em>M. luteus</em> typically has two corresponding homoeologs encoded on subgenomes (<em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and <em>L<sub>b</sub></em>). The mean per base divergence at neutral sites between the two <em>M. luteus</em> subgenomes is ∼0.11 Ks (synonymous substitutions per synonymous site). This level of divergence supports previous claim that this species is an allopolyploid (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Mukherjee and Vickery, 1962</a>) and by our previous genomic analysis, which suggested an allopolyploid origin for this taxon (<a id="xref-ref-93-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">Vallejo-Marín et al., 2015</a>, <a id="xref-ref-92-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">2016</a>). Over 50 years ago, <a id="xref-ref-60-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Mukherjee and Vickery (1962)</a> hypothesized that <em>M. luteus</em> may be an allopolyploid formed by the hybridization of two distinct species with one more closely related to <em>M. guttatus</em> than the other species (<a id="xref-ref-60-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Mukherjee and Vickery, 1962</a>). We sought to test this hypothesis with phylogenetic analyses of syntenic orthologs between <em>M.</em> <em>guttatus</em> and from both <em>M. luteus</em> subgenomes. We determine that in the majority of cases (1853 of 2200 gene trees), homoeologs encoded on subgenome A were more closely related to <em>M. guttatus</em> than it was to other subgenome supporting their hypothesis. However, it is important to note the <em>M. luteus</em> subgenomes are both quite distinct from <em>M. guttatus</em> at the sequence level (genome-wide average = 91.52% sequence similarity across genes). This suggests that the diploid progenitor species of either subgenome is likely not a close relative of <em>M. guttatus</em>, but rather a more distantly related species in the past. Thus, the newly formed allopolyploid <em>M. peregrinus</em> consists of three unique subgenomes; two from <em>M. luteus</em> (<em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and <em>L<sub>b</sub></em>) and one from <em>M. guttatus</em>.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Investigating the Establishment of Subgenome Dominance</h3><p id="p-25">The goal of this study was to determine whether homoeologs from a given subgenome are expressed at higher levels than homoeologs from another subgenome. This goal guided our sampling strategy and analysis approach. Specifically, we sought to determine whether one homoeolog was the dominantly expressed form over multiple tissue types (our goal is not to determine tissue-specific expression bias).</p><p id="p-26">Toward this end, for each individual represented in this study (parents, hybrid, and natural allopolyploids), three separate RNA-seq data sets derived from three different tissues were generated. RNA-seq data sets for calyx, stem, and petals were generated for the F1 hybrid <em>M. x robertsii</em> (<em>M. guttatus</em> x <em>M. luteus</em>), the resynthesized allopolyploid <em>M. peregrinus</em>, and the naturally derived allopolyploid <em>M. peregrinus</em> and parental taxa, <em>M. luteus</em> and <em>M. guttatus</em>. For each tissue type, we calculated the expression ratios of homoeologs encoded on separate subgenomes. Thus, our three biological replicates were the expression ratio of homoeologs across three tissues. These data were used to measure homoeolog-specific gene expression following the <em>M. luteus</em> <a id="xref-def-1-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> event as well as in a contemporary hybrid and neo-allopolyploids (<em>M. peregrinus</em>). All RNA samples were collected within a narrow time range to control for major diurnal rhythmic expression differences. In hybrids and allopolyploids, subgenome-specific (parental) single-nucleotide polymorphisms were identified and used to measure homoeolog-specific gene expression. For the analysis of our RNA-seq data, we developed a likelihood ratio test (<a id="xref-def-3-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">LRT</a>) involving three nested hypotheses to identify cases of homoeolog expression bias that do not involve tissue-specific expression differences. The null hypothesis is that both homoeologs are expressed at equal levels (ratio of homoeolog-1 to homoeolog-2 equals 1 for all three tissues). The first alternate hypothesis is that homoeologs are expressed at different levels, but similar ratios, across all three tissue types. The second alternate hypothesis is that homoeologs are expressed at different levels and at different ratios across all three tissues.</p><p id="p-27">Using the expression data and the nested hypotheses we test for (1) expression bias and subgenome dominance following the <em>M. luteus</em>-specific <a id="xref-def-1-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a>, comparing <em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and <em>L<sub>b</sub></em> homoeolog expression, in <em>M. luteus</em>, hybrid <em>M. guttatus x M. luteus</em>, and <em>M. peregrinus</em> (both natural and resynthesized allopolyploid) and (2) expression bias and subgenome dominance of the <em>M. guttatus</em> or <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs in the hybrid and allopolyploid <em>M. peregrinus</em>. Importantly, the first comparison will allow us to understand long-term patterns of expression bias in <em>Mimulus</em> (the <em>M. luteus</em> <a id="xref-def-1-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> is a relatively ancient event), whereas the second comparison will test for expression bias and subgenome dominance in a newly formed hybrid and allopolyploid <em>Mimulus</em>.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Expression Bias of Homoeologs from <em>M. luteus</em>-Specific <a id="xref-def-1-16" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> Event</h3><p id="p-28">We sought to compare expression of homoeologs within <em>M. luteus</em> to each other, thereby addressing the question of whether <em>L<sub>a</sub></em> homoeologs or <em>L<sub>b</sub></em> homoeologs are more highly expressed within <em>M. luteus</em>, <em>M. x robertsii</em> (F1 hybrid), or <em>M. peregrinus</em> (both a resynthesized and natural allopolyploid). Using the likelihood ratio test mentioned above, we identified cases of homoeolog expression bias that did not involve tissue-specific expression differences. In each case, over 1100 homoeolog pairs were tested; the test was only applied when both homoeologs were expressed in all three tissues. For each homoeolog pair, we quantified expression bias as</p><span class="disp-formula" id="disp-formula-1"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/graphic-2.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/graphic-2.gif"/></noscript></span></span><p id="p-29">where the subscripts <em>a</em> and <em>b</em> denote the two distinct subgenomes (<em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and <em>L<sub>b</sub></em>), and <em>j</em> is an index over the <em>N</em> tissues. An expression bias of <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-1"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-1.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-1.gif"/></noscript></span></span> = <em>−</em>2 indicates a 4x expression bias toward the “<em>a</em>” homoeolog, while an expression bias of <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-2"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-2.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-2.gif"/></noscript></span></span>= 3 is 8x toward the “b” homoeolog. The histograms in <a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2</a> summarize the measured expression bias of homoeologs resulting from the <em>M. luteus</em>-specific <a id="xref-def-1-17" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">WGD</a> event in <em>M. luteus</em>, F1 hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and natural allopolyploid. For <em>M. luteus</em> (top panel), the gray histogram shows the distribution of expression bias for all testable homoeolog pairs indicating a slight average bias toward the <em>L<sub>a</sub></em> subgenome (<span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-3"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-3.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-3.gif"/></noscript></span></span> = <em>−</em>0.09, <em>NL<sub>a</sub></em> = 388 <em>&gt;</em> 336 = <em>NL<sub>b</sub></em>) (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2</a>). Using the likelihood ratio test developed in this article, we found that over half of the homoeolog pairs in <em>M. luteus</em> (<em>NL<sub>a</sub></em> + <em>NL<sub>b</sub></em> = 724, ∼53% of the total <em>N</em>) were biased toward one of the subgenomes with no tissue-specific expression differences (hypothesis one; see Methods). Of these, a small majority of homoeologs (<em>NL<sub>a</sub></em> = 388, ∼54% of biased homoeologs) were dominantly expressed from the <em>L<sub>a</sub></em> subgenome. In contrast to <em>M. luteus</em>, in the hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and natural allopolyploid, the <em>L<sub>b</sub></em> subgenome is slightly dominant in either number or average (see <a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2</a>, where ∼52, 51, and 53% of biased homoeologs are dominantly expressed from the <em>L<sub>b</sub></em> subgenome and <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-4"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-4.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-4.gif"/></noscript></span></span> = 0.05, 0.01, and 0.02, respectively).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Expression Bias of Homoeologs Resulting from M. luteus-Specific WGD Event in M. luteus, F1 Hybrid, Synthetic Allopolyploid, and Natural Allopolyploid. pairs that were biased toward <em>L<sub>b</sub></em> or <em>L<sub>a</sub></em>, respectively.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Expression Bias of <em>M. guttatus</em> and <em>M. luteus</em> Homoeologs in the Hybrid, Resynthesized Allopolyploid, and Naturally Occurring Neo-Allopolyploid <em>Mimulus</em></h3><p id="p-32">To test for expression bias that arises instantaneously following the merger of two genomes, we compared homoeologs in the hybrid, resynthesized allopolyploid, and natural allopolyploid, which contain both a <em>M. guttatus</em> and <em>M. luteus</em> subgenome. We asked two questions. First, when considering <em>M. luteus</em> weighted average of its homoeologs compared with <em>M. guttatus</em>, do we see expression dominance toward either species (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3</a>)? Second, when we consider the <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs separately (<em>L<sub>b</sub></em> or <em>L<sub>a</sub></em>) and compare these to their <em>M. guttatus</em> homoeolog (<em>G</em>), do we see expression dominance toward either species or a particular subgenome? The weighted average of expression of the two <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs was calculated by dividing the sum of read count of the two <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs by the sum of their individual gene lengths. The answer to the first question may indicate which parental species overall is more expressed, while the second question will shed light whether one single subgenome is most dominant. For each homoeolog pair, we quantified expression bias as</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Homoeolog Expression Bias in Hybrid and Allopolyploids, Comparing the M. guttatus Homoeolog to the Weighted Average Expression of Its Pair of M. luteus Homoeologs. The weighted average of expression of the two M. luteus homoeologs was calculated by dividing the sum of read count of the two M. luteus homoeologs by the sum of their individual gene lengths. Gray histograms show distribution of expression bias () for all testable homoeolog pairs. Only genes that had a clear 2 to 1 (M. luteus to M. guttatus) homology were considered. Homoeolog pairs significantly biased toward the M. guttatus homoeolog are shown in yellow, while pairs significantly biased toward the M. luteus homoeolog are shown in blue. Across all three hybrid individuals (F1, synthetic, and natural allopolyploid) the pair of M. luteus homoeologs, when added together, dominates the M. guttatus homoeolog (i.e.,  and , where  and  are averages over all homoeolog pairs)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-351170126" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Homoeolog Expression Bias in Hybrid and Allopolyploids, Comparing the &lt;em&gt;M. guttatus&lt;/em&gt; Homoeolog to the Weighted Average Expression of Its Pair of &lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; Homoeologs. The weighted average of expression of the two &lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; homoeologs was calculated by dividing the sum of read count of the two &lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; homoeologs by the sum of their individual gene lengths. Gray histograms show distribution of expression bias () for all testable homoeolog pairs. Only genes that had a clear 2 to 1 (&lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; to &lt;em&gt;M. guttatus&lt;/em&gt;) homology were considered. Homoeolog pairs significantly biased toward the &lt;em&gt;M. guttatus&lt;/em&gt; homoeolog are shown in yellow, while pairs significantly biased toward the &lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; homoeolog are shown in blue. Across all three hybrid individuals (F1, synthetic, and natural allopolyploid) the pair of &lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; homoeologs, when added together, dominates the &lt;em&gt;M. guttatus&lt;/em&gt; homoeolog (i.e.,  and , where  and  are averages over all homoeolog pairs).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3.medium.gif" width="400" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 3." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3.medium.gif" width="400" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/68643" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 3.</span> <p id="p-33" class="first-child">Homoeolog Expression Bias in Hybrid and Allopolyploids, Comparing the <em>M. guttatus</em> Homoeolog to the Weighted Average Expression of Its Pair of <em>M. luteus</em> Homoeologs.</p><p id="p-34">The weighted average of expression of the two <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs was calculated by dividing the sum of read count of the two <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs by the sum of their individual gene lengths. Gray histograms show distribution of expression bias (<span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-9"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3/embed/inline-graphic-9.gif.backup.1507659685.0434"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3/embed/inline-graphic-9.gif.backup.1507659685.0434"/></noscript></span></span>) for all testable homoeolog pairs. Only genes that had a clear 2 to 1 (<em>M. luteus</em> to <em>M. guttatus</em>) homology were considered. Homoeolog pairs significantly biased toward the <em>M. guttatus</em> homoeolog are shown in yellow, while pairs significantly biased toward the <em>M. luteus</em> homoeolog are shown in blue. Across all three hybrid individuals (F1, synthetic, and natural allopolyploid) the pair of <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs, when added together, dominates the <em>M. guttatus</em> homoeolog (i.e., <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-10"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3/embed/inline-graphic-10.gif.backup.1507659685.0739"/></noscript></span></span> and <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-11"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3/embed/inline-graphic-11.gif.backup.1507659685.1054"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-12"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed id="inline-formula-13"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/F3/embed/inline-graphic-13.gif.backup.1507659685.1716"/></noscript></span></span> are averages over all homoeolog pairs).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><span class="disp-formula" id="disp-formula-2"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/graphic-5.gif"/></noscript></span></span><p id="p-35">where the subscripts <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-14"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-14.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-14.gif"/></noscript></span></span> and <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-15"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" respectively, and <em>j</em> is an index over the <em>N</em> tissues. Considering the <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs separately allows us to control for additive expression levels.</p><p id="p-36">The histograms in <a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3</a> summarize the measured expression bias in hybrid and allopolyploids, comparing the expression of the <em>M. guttatus</em> homoeolog to the weighted average expression of its pair of <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs. This weighted average of expression and is a proxy for the average expression activity of homoeolog pairs from the <em>M. luteus</em> genome. On average, there is considerable bias toward the <em>M. luteus</em> subgenome with <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-16"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-16.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-16.gif"/></noscript></span></span> = 0.57, 0.51, and 1.05 in the first generation hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and natural allopolyploid, respectively. Using the <a id="xref-def-3-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">LRT</a> (at significance level α = 0.01), a total of 1893 (57%), 2072 (66%), and 2096 (70%) homoeolog pairs were found to be significantly biased. Of these biased pairs, the <em>M. luteus</em> homoeolog was dominant in the vast majority of cases (<span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-17"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-17.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-17.gif"/></noscript></span></span> = 1330 (70%), 1397 (67%), and 1573 (75%), respectively).</p><p id="p-37">The histograms in <a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a> summarize the measured expression bias in hybrid and allopolyploids, comparing the expression of the <em>M. guttatus</em> homoeolog to expression of each of its <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs separately. That is, for each <em>M. guttatus</em> gene (G), there are two <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs, <em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and <em>L<sub>b</sub></em>. <a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a> includes the comparisons: G versus <em>L<sub>a</sub></em> and G versus <em>L<sub>b</sub></em>. In other words, each G is used in two different comparisons. When considering the <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs separately, expression is still considerably biased on average toward <em>M. luteus</em> with <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-18"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-18.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-18.gif"/></noscript></span></span> = 0.51, 0.48, and 1.00, in the first generation hybrid, resynthesized allopolyploid, and natural allopolyploid, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>). Next, using the <a id="xref-def-3-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">LRT</a>, across all comparisons, 55, 64, and 69% homoeologs were significantly biased. Of these biased homoeolog pairs, the <em>M. luteus</em> homoeolog was the dominantly expressed homoeolog in 69, 65, and 74% of the comparisons (<a id="xref-fig-4-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>). Additionally, among biased homoeologs, the average bias toward the <em>M. luteus</em> subgenome is greater than the average bias toward the <em>M. guttatus</em> subgenome <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-19"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-19.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/embed/inline-graphic-19.gif"/></noscript></span></span>in all cases (<a id="xref-fig-4-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>). It is also worth noting that the degree of bias (as measured by the fraction of biased homoeologs and <em>B</em> of biased homoeologs) increased from the first generation hybrid, to the resynthesized allopolyploid, and to the natural allopolyploid.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Homoeolog Expression Bias in Hybrid and Allopolyploids, Comparing the M. guttatus Homoeolog to Each of Its M. luteus Homoeologs Separately. pairs).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Expression Bias in Three Separate Hybrid Lineages</h3><p id="p-40">While it is clear that the <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs are dominantly expressed in the hybrid and allopolyploid lineages, we sought to determine whether the same homoeologs were repeatedly biased across independent hybrid and allopolyploids. A Venn diagram reveals that homoeologs biased in one individual are far more likely to be biased in the other two lineages than would be expected by random chance (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>). Moreover, measured levels of individual homoeolog expression bias are correlated across all three lineages (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>). Interestingly, levels of expression bias <em>B</em> in the first generation hybrid and resynthesized allopolyploid are much more correlated with each other (<em>r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.70) than either sample is with the natural allopolyploid (<em>r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.35 and 0.33; <a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>).</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Expression Bias in Three Separate Hybrid Lineages. (A) Venn diagram of the number of biased homoeolog pairs across hybrid lineages (1532 homoeolog pairs were biased in all three lineages). (B) to (D) Scatterplots of expression bias () for these 1532 homoeolog pairs comparing hybrid to synthetic allopolyploid, hybrid to natural allopolyploid, and synthetic to natural allopolyploid (red line is linear regression; thin blue line is identity)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-351170126" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Expression Bias in Three Separate Hybrid Lineages. &lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Venn diagram of the 1532 homoeolog pairs comparing hybrid to synthetic allopolyploid, hybrid to natural allopolyploid, and synthetic to natural allopolyploid (red line is linear regression; thin blue line is identity).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 5." data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 5.</span> <p id="p-41" class="first-child">Expression Bias in Three Separate Hybrid Lineages.</p><p id="p-42"><strong>(A)</strong> Venn diagram of the number of biased homoeolog pairs across hybrid lineages (1532 homoeolog pairs were biased in all three lineages).</p><p id="p-43"><strong>(B)</strong> to (D) Scatterplots of expression bias (<span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-25"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/F5/embed/inline-graphic-25.gif.backup.1507659685.6147"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/29/9/2150/F5/embed/inline-graphic-25.gif.backup.1507659685.6147"/></noscript></span></span>) for these 1532 homoeolog pairs comparing hybrid to synthetic allopolyploid, hybrid to natural allopolyploid, and synthetic to natural allopolyploid (red line is linear regression; thin blue line is identity).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Transposon Density Linked to Gene Expression</h3><p id="p-44">One possibility we considered was whether proximal transposon (<a id="xref-def-2-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a>) loads were related to homoeolog expression bias. In order to test this, it was necessary to annotate TEs in <em>M. guttatus</em> and <em>M. luteus</em> genome assemblies. Using a homology and structured based annotation, as well as de novo annotation, we identified the transposons in the <em>M. guttatus</em> and <em>M. luteus</em> genomes. Our survey revealed that 50% of the <em>M. guttatus</em> genome assembly is composed of <a id="xref-def-2-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> sequences that are classified into 863 families. We compiled a TE exemplar library with 1439 sequences representing the TE composition of the genome. After annotating TEs in 10-kb windows (10 kb upstream and downstream of genes as well as within genic region), we calculated the total number of TEs and the number of TE bases. On average, <em>M. luteus</em> homoeologs and <em>M. guttatus</em> homoeologs have TE densities of 0.31 and 0.34 (fraction of bases that occur within a transposon), respectively. In the parents, hybrid, and allopolyploid individuals, this measure of proximal TE density is negatively correlated with gene expression (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 6</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.17.00010/DC1">Supplemental Figures 6 and 7</a>).</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="TE Density in a Window Spanning 10 kb Upstream to 10 kb Downstream of a Gene Is Negatively Related to Gene Expression. The vertical axis is gene expression in RPKM. The horizontal axis is transposon density, binned into 10 windows with width proportional to the number of data points it contains. Horizontal gray line indicates the mean of the response, log(RPKM). TE density is negatively related to gene expression in M. guttatus (A) and M. luteus (B)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-351170126" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;TE Density in a Window Spanning 10 kb Upstream to 10 kb Downstream of a Gene Is Negatively Related to Gene Expression. The vertical axis is gene expression in RPKM. The horizontal axis is transposon density, binned into 10 windows with width proportional to the number of data points it contains. Horizontal gray line indicates the mean of the response, log(RPKM). TE density is negatively related to gene expression in &lt;em&gt;M. guttatus&lt;/em&gt; &lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; and &lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; &lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F6.medium.gif" width="385" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 6." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F6.medium.gif" width="385" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/68569" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 6.</span> <p id="p-45" class="first-child"><a id="xref-def-2-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> Density in a Window Spanning 10 kb Upstream to 10 kb Downstream of a Gene Is Negatively Related to Gene Expression.</p><p id="p-46">The vertical axis is gene expression in RPKM. The horizontal axis is transposon density, binned into 10 windows with width proportional to the number of data points it contains. Horizontal gray line indicates the mean of the response, log(RPKM). <a id="xref-def-2-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">TE</a> density is negatively related to gene expression in <em>M. guttatus</em> <strong>(A)</strong> and <em>M. luteus</em> <strong>(B)</strong>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Altered DNA Methylation Patterns in Parents, Hybrid, and Allopolyploids</h3><p id="p-47">Building on the finding of expression bias in the hybrid and neo-allopolyploids, we asked whether DNA methylation changes mirror the observed expression bias. First, whole-genome bisulfite sequencing was used to determine the methylation status and patterns of methylation change in hybrid and neo-allopolyploid lineages as well as in each parent at CHH (where H = C, A, T), CHG, and CG sites. Next, we tested the hypothesis that changes in TE methylation between parents and hybrid or allopolyploids may explain patterns of subgenome dominance.</p><p id="p-48">Methylation patterns in TE and genes and their upstream and downstream regions were compared. CG and CHG methylation patterns in genes and TE are unchanged in the hybrid and allopolyploid lineages (<a id="xref-fig-7-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 7</a>). CHG methylation levels are marginally lower in upstream and downstream regions of genes in the hybrid and slightly higher in the synthetic and natural allopolyploid. CHH methylation levels are decreased significantly in upstream and downstream genic regions in the first generation hybrid, decreased slightly in the resynthesized allopolyploid, and returned to parental levels in the natural allopolyploid. Similar to findings in genes, transposon bodies and up- and downstream regions of transposons are depleted in CHH methylation in the hybrid and synthetic allopolyploid (<a id="xref-fig-7-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Figure 7</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.17.00010/DC1">Supplemental Figure 8</a>). Transposon CHH methylation levels are lowest in the first generation hybrid and remained at a reduced level in the resynthesized allopolyploid compared with parental levels. In the natural allopolyploid, CHH methylation of TEs across the <em>M. guttatus</em> subgenome returned to near parental levels while <em>M. luteus</em> subgenome TE methylation remained lower compared with parental levels. Methylation repatterning across the subgenomes closely reflects the pattern of homoeolog expression bias observed between the two subgenomes in the hybrid and allopolyploids.</p><div id="F7" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/29/9/2150/F7.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Subgenome-Specific Methylation Repatterning in Hybrid and Allopolyploid Mimulus. M. guttatus and M. luteus subgenome-specific patterns of gene (top two rows) and transposon (bottom two rows) methylation. The y axis is the weighted methylation level. The x axis shows the gene body (TSS = transcription start site and TTS = transcription termination site) or TE body and 1 kb upstream and downstream. CG, CHG, and CHH methylation levels are shown in the first, second, and third column, respectively. Methylation levels of each individual are shown in unique colors (parents = red; F1 hybrid = light blue; synthetic allopolyploid = dark green; natural allopolyploid = yellow)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-351170126" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Subgenome-Specific Methylation Repatterning in Hybrid and Allopolyploid &lt;em&gt;Mimulus&lt;/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;M. guttatus&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;M. luteus&lt;/em&gt; subgenome-specific patterns of gene (top two rows) and transposon (bottom two rows) methylation.
